</div></section></section><section aria-label="Article content"><section class="body main-article-body"><section class="abstract" id="Abs1"><h2>Abstract</h2>
<p id="Par1">During the construction and assembly of the Mars 2020 mission components at two different NASA cleanrooms, several fungal strains were isolated. Based on their colony morphology, two strains that showed yeast-like appearance were further characterized for their phylogenetic position. The species-level classification of these two novel strains, using traditional colony and cell morphology methods combined with the phylogenetic reconstructions using multi-locus sequence analysis (MLSA) based on several gene loci (ITS, LSU, SSU, <em>RPB</em>1, <em>RPB2</em>, <em>CYTB</em> and <em>TEF1</em>), and whole genome sequencing (WGS) was carried out. This polyphasic taxonomic approach supported the conclusion that the two basidiomycetous yeasts belong to hitherto undescribed species. The strain FJI-L2-BK-P3<sup>T</sup>, isolated from the Jet Propulsion Laboratory Spacecraft Assembly Facility, was placed in the <em>Naganishia albida</em> clade<em> (Filobasidiales, Tremellomycetes),</em> but is genetically and physiologically different from other members of the clade. Another yeast strain FKI-L6-BK-PAB1<sup>T</sup>, isolated from the Kennedy Space Center Payload Hazardous and Servicing Facility, was placed in the genus <em>Cystobasidium</em> (<em>Cystobasidiales, Cystobasidiomycetes</em>) and is distantly related to <em>C. benthicum.</em> Here we propose two novel species with the type strains, <em>Naganishia kalamii</em> sp. nov. (FJI-L2-BK-P3<sup>T</sup> = NRRL 64466 = DSM 115730) and <em>Cystobasidium onofrii</em> sp. nov. (FKI-L6-BK-PAB1<sup>T</sup> = NRRL 64426 = DSM 114625). The phylogenetic analyses revealed that single gene phylogenies (ITS or LSU) were not conclusive, and MLSA and WGS-based phylogenies were more advantageous for species discrimination in the two genera. The genomic analysis predicted proteins associated with dehydration and desiccation stress-response and the presence of genes that are directly related to osmotolerance and psychrotolerance in both novel yeasts described. Cells of these two newly-described yeasts were exposed to UV-C radiation and compared with <em>N. onofrii,</em> an extremophilic UV-C resistant cold-adapted Alpine yeast. Both novel species were UV resistant, emphasizing the need for collecting and characterizing extremotolerant microbes, including yeasts, to improve microbial reduction techniques used in NASA planetary protection programs.</p>
<section id="sec1"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Supplementary Information</h3>
<p>The online version contains supplementary material available at 10.1186/s43008-023-00119-4.</p></section><section id="kwd-group1" lang="en" class="kwd-group"><p><strong>Keywords:</strong> 2 new species, <em>Naganishia kalamii</em>, <em>Cystobasidium onofrii</em>, Mars 2020 mission, NASA cleanrooms, UV resistance, Extremophiles</p></section></section><section id="Sec1"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">INTRODUCTION</h2>
<p id="Par72">Many fungal species, including yeasts, have been isolated and characterized from different extreme environments, such as the Antarctic continent (rocks, soil brines), Arctic ice-sheet and sea-ice, deep sea, deserts, etc. (Buzzini and Margesin <a href="#CR14" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR14">2014</a>; Segal-Kischinevzky et al. <a href="#CR83" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR83">2022</a>; Coleine et al. <a href="#CR22" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR22">2022</a>). Yeasts and yeast-like fungi isolated from Antarctic environments (proposed as resembling the surface and conditions of Mars and/or Icy Moons) were reported to successfully resist a wide spectrum of stressors which mimic conditions on other planetary bodies, including ionizing radiation, UV-flux irradiation, extremely low and high temperatures, desiccation, high salinity and acidic conditions (Onofri et al. <a href="#CR67" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR67">2004</a>, <a href="#CR68" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR68">2020</a>; Selbmann et al. <a href="#CR84" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR84">2005</a>; Pacelli et al. <a href="#CR69" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR69">2017</a>, <a href="#CR70" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR70">2021</a>; Buzzini et al. <a href="#CR13" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR13">2018</a>). Since National Aeronautics and Space Administration (NASA) Planetary Protection (PP) seeks to prevent anthropogenic microbial transport (forward contamination) to other planets (Cockell <a href="#CR21" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR21">2005</a>; Conley and Rummel <a href="#CR23" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR23">2010</a>), evaluation of hitchhiking extremophilic yeasts on spacecraft and associated components is necessary.</p>
<p id="Par73">A wide range of microbial taxa, including both bacterial and fungal species, have been detected in various NASA spacecraft assembly cleanrooms (La Duc et al. <a href="#CR50" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR50">2012</a>), but historically NASA and European Space Agency (ESA) PP policy has focused on monitoring and eradicating bacteria, specifically those producing endospores. However, the presence of fungi in these cleanrooms has also recently been assessed, since fungi are tolerant of a wide range of extreme conditions (Parker et al. <a href="#CR72" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR72">2022</a>; Blachowicz et al. <a href="#CR9" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR9">2022</a>; Chander et al. <a href="#CR18" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR18">2022b</a>). Characterization of fungi (including yeast) is important both for assessing the risk of forward contamination and updating appropriate cleaning and sterilization strategies (Benardini and Moissl-Eichinger <a href="#CR5" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR5">2022</a>). Therefore, newly discovered fungal (including yeasts) species in cleanrooms must be evaluated not only for their morphology, but also at the genomic level so that new countermeasures can be developed to eradicate them based on their biology and resilient properties (Chander et al. <a href="#CR18" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR18">2022b</a>).</p>
<p id="Par74">To date, only filamentous fungi have been described from cleanrooms (Parker et al. <a href="#CR72" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR72">2022</a>; Blachowicz et al. <a href="#CR9" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR9">2022</a>; Chander et al. <a href="#CR18" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR18">2022b</a>). In contrast to filamentous fungi, yeasts are microscopic and predominantly unicellular (Boekhout et al. <a href="#CR12" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR12">2021</a>). It has been shown that yeast and yeast-like fungi are capable of adapting to and colonizing various ecological niches due to their genetic and phenotypic plasticity and robustness (Ene et al. <a href="#CR29" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR29">2015</a>; Ribeiro et al. <a href="#CR79" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR79">2022</a>). Several important adaptations facilitate survival of yeast cells in harsh environments, including production of thick external polysaccharide capsules, forming biofilm, and UV protective compounds, melanin, carotenoids and mycosporines (Buzzini et al. <a href="#CR13" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR13">2018</a>). In this study, we describe for the first time two novel yeast species from NASA cleanrooms, which are oligotrophic extreme environments.</p>
<p id="Par75">To explore yeast diversity in various environments including NASA cleanrooms, understanding their phylogenetic diversity and ecology is necessary. Phylogenies based on 16S ribosomal RNA or average nucleotide identity (ANI) are most commonly used for prokaryote taxonomy, but fungal species are identified with nucleotide sequences of multiple conserved gene regions (Lücking et al. <a href="#CR59" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR59">2021</a>). To identify yeasts, both phenotypic traits and molecular analyses are required (Boekhout et al. <a href="#CR12" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR12">2021</a>). The modern yeast taxonomy includes genealogical approaches based on single locus (ITS or LSU), multi-locus sequence analysis (MLSA) and recently whole genome sequencing (WGS) (Libkind et al. <a href="#CR55" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR55">2020</a>; Boekhout et al. <a href="#CR12" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR12">2021</a>; Chander et al. <a href="#CR18" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR18">2022b</a>). Molecular phylogeny for species recognition in Fungi is gaining broad attention (Lücking et al. <a href="#CR59" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR59">2021</a>), but understanding species ecology requires investigating morphology, physiology, and biochemistry leading to the Consolidated Species Concept based on the so-called polyphasic approach (Boekhout et al. <a href="#CR12" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR12">2021</a>).</p>
<p id="Par76">One of the objectives of this study was to characterize two novel yeast species by employing the polyphasic taxonomic approach, which is necessary to formally describe the two novel taxa. Colony morphology, microscopic features, and physiological properties were combined with MLSA phylogeny (seven loci). In addition, a phylogenetic analysis based on available WGS was applied to test its utility for species discrimination. The assembled and characterized genomes of <em>Naganishia kalamii</em> FJI-L2-BK-P3<sup>T</sup> and <em>Cystobasidium onofrii</em> FKI-L6-BK-PAB1<sup>T</sup> were compared with genomes of other closely related species to identify unique and shared evolutionary characters. Considering unique properties of the habitat challenging the microbial life, the third objective of this study was to assess the UV-C resistance of the two novel yeast species and compared with a UV-C resistant extremophilic yeast, <em>Naganishia onofrii</em> strain DBVPG 5303 from Miage glacier (Mont Blanc, Alps, Italy) at the altitude of 3892 m above sea level (Turchetti et al. <a href="#CR95" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR95">2015</a>).</p></section><section id="Sec2"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">MATERIALS AND METHODS</h2>
<section id="Sec3"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Sample collection and isolation of yeasts</h3>
<p id="Par77">Steps for sample collection and isolation of yeasts at the Jet Propulsion Laboratory Spacecraft Assembly Facility (JPL-SAF) and the Kennedy Space Center Payload Hazardous and Servicing Facility (KSC-PHSF) cleanrooms were previously described (Parker et al. <a href="#CR72" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR72">2022</a>; Blachowicz et al. <a href="#CR9" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR9">2022</a>). Briefly, samples were collected from cleanroom surfaces using moist polyester wipes and were agitated in 200 mL sterile phosphate-buffered saline (PBS) and shaken for 1 min. The resulting PBS suspension was concentrated using an Innova Prep LLC concentrating pipette and spread onto plates containing potato dextrose agar (PDA, Difco, #213,400) mixed with chloramphenicol (25 µg/mL). Plates were incubated at 25 °C for 7–14 days, and yeast strains were isolated, purified, and stored for further characterization.</p>
<p id="Par78">Steps for determination of microscopic traits and observation and microscopic morphology were followed as per previous protocol (Chander et al. <a href="#CR18" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR18">2022b</a>). In a nutshell, a small block of agar was placed in the center of a sterile slide, and all four sides of the agar were inoculated with the yeast suspension. Subsequently, a sterile cover slip was gently placed on top of the agar block. The slide culture was kept in a moist Petri dish lined with filter paper soaked in sterile water. After 14–21 days, the yeast grew out onto the coverslip and the slide was tested. The cover slip was gently removed with sterile forceps and placed on a clean microscopic slide with a drop of water or lactophenol cotton blue (Hardy Diagnostics, Santa Maria, CA) for microscopic observations. Phase contrast images were captured on an Olympus BX53 microscope with an Olympus DP25 camera and Olympus cellSens software. Differential interference contrast (DIC) images were generated on a Nikon Ti2-E inverted microscope with Nikon NIS Elements software. Cell morphological characters were measured with the Olympus cellSens or Nikon NIS Elements software.</p>
<p id="Par79">Assimilation tests were performed in liquid media following the procedures described by (Kurtzman et al. <a href="#CR49" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR49">2011</a>), in custom-made microplates (Nunc 96-Well U-Bottom plate, Thermo Fisher Scientific) and 5 mL tubes (Falcon, Corning) using the same standard set of substrates and with YT MicroPlates (BioLog) as described elsewhere (Greetham et al. <a href="#CR37" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR37">2014</a>; Mašínová et al. <a href="#CR61" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR61">2018</a>; Passer et al. <a href="#CR73" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR73">2019</a>). Tubes and microplates with suitable aliquots of test cultures were incubated at 25 °C. Utilization of substrates was examined in PDA and incubated for 7 days. The change in coloration and oxidation and/or assimilation profiles of 94 different carbon sources were documented by visual examination. The clear positives and negatives were scored within 7 days of incubation, and delayed reaction (weak) was documented by examining the tetrazolium violet color change after 14 days of incubation. Further incubation and observation on 14th day did not yield any difference in results compared to those that were observed at day 7. Tubes were examined after 7, 14 and 21 days, and the growth (changing turbidity) was documented by visual examination.</p></section><section id="Sec4"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Preliminary identification based on ITS sequencing</h3>
<p id="Par80">DNA was extracted from 7-day grown yeast cultures on PDA incubated at 25 °C using the tissue LEV purification kit with Maxwell-16 MDx automated system (Promega, Madison, WI, USA). The ITS region was amplified employing polymerase chain reaction (PCR), using primers ITS 1F (5′-CTT GGT CAT TTA GAG GAA GTA A-3′) (Lai et al. <a href="#CR51" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR51">2007</a>)<em>,</em> and Tw13 (5′-GGT CCG TGT TTC AAG ACG-3′) (Taylor and Bruns <a href="#CR90" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR90">1999</a>). PCR conditions and sample preparation steps for sequencing are described elsewhere (Blachowicz et al. <a href="#CR7" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR7">2017</a>)<em>.</em> The ITS sequences were also included in the subsequent MLSA-based analyses.</p></section><section id="Sec5"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Whole genome sequencing</h3>
<p id="Par81">Genomic DNA was isolated from 1 g wet biomass scraped from PDA plates, using a ZymoBIOMICS MagBead DNA kit (Zymo Research) and bead beating, followed by a Precellys homogenizer (Bertin) treatment. The quality of genomic DNA was verified by gel electrophoresis and quantified by spectrophotometric measurements (Nanophotometer NP80 Mobile, Implen GmbH). Library preparation was performed using the Illumina Nextera Flex Protocol (Illumina document # 1000000025416 v07). The initial amount of DNA for library preparation was quantified, and 5–12 cycles of PCR amplification were carried out to normalize the output depending on the input DNA concentration. The amplified genomic DNA fragments were indexed and pooled in a 384-plex configuration with dual-index adapters. WGS was performed on a NovaSeq 6000 S4 flowcell PE 2 × 150 base pairs (bp) platform with a paired-end module. The data was filtered with NGS QC Toolkit v2.3 (Patel and Jain <a href="#CR74" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR74">2012</a>)<em>,</em> for high-quality (HQ) vector and adaptor-free reads for genome assembly (cutoff read length for HQ, 80%; cutoff quality score, 20). The genomes for the two novel yeast species were reassembled using the Automatic Assembly For The Fungi (AAFTF v 0.3.3) pipeline (Stajich and Palmer <a href="#CR88" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR88">2019</a>), as follows: (i)The Illumina sequencing reads underwent trimming via the BBDuk module within the BBMap software suite (v 38.95<em>)</em> (BBMap: A Fast, Accurate, Splice-Aware Aligner (Conference) OSTI.GOV); (ii) BBMap was employed to eliminate any contaminant reads, and (iii) the resulting high-quality reads were assembled using SPAdes v3.15.4 (Bankevich et al. <a href="#CR4" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR4">2012</a>). (iv) Contaminant contigs were identified and removed, using nucleotide Basic Local Alignment Search Tool (BLASTn) (Johnson et al. <a href="#CR45" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR45">2008</a>); (v) We purged duplicated contigs using minimap2 (Li <a href="#CR53" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR53">2018</a>). Final assemblies were polished, using the Pilon v1.24 software (Walker et al. <a href="#CR99" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR99">2014</a>); (vii) The final scaffolds were sorted by length and the fasta headers renamed for further annotation. The assembly quality was verified with QUAST 5.1.0 (Gurevich et al. <a href="#CR39" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR39">2013</a>) and completeness using BUSCO and its basidiomycota_odb10 database (Seppey et al. <a href="#CR86" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR86">2019</a>).</p></section><section id="Sec6"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Genome annotation and comparative genomic analyses</h3>
<p id="Par82">As described in Chander et al. (<a href="#CR18" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR18">2022b</a>), the funannotate v1.8 pipeline (Palmer and Stajich <a href="#CR71" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR71">2020</a>) for fungal genome annotation was used. Masked repetitive DNA content were identified using TANTAN (Frith <a href="#CR34" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR34">2011</a>) using the funannotate mask command. The funnanotate predict command was used for gene content prediction, via both evidence-based and ab initio (i.e. structure-based) prediction pathways. The basidiomycota_odb10 BUSCO database (Simão et al. <a href="#CR87" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR87">2015</a>) was used to find conserved gene models for training the ab initio predictors Augustus (Seppey et al. <a href="#CR86" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR86">2019</a>), glimmerhmm (Delcher <a href="#CR26" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR26">1999</a>), and snap (Korf <a href="#CR47" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR47">2004</a>). The GeneMark-ES was also evoked with the option for fungal genomes (Lomsadze <a href="#CR58" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR58">2005</a>). Weighted consensus gene structure was obtained via EVidenceModeler (Haas et al. <a href="#CR40" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR40">2008</a>) using weigh = 2 in the case of Augustus HiQ models and weight = 1 otherwise. Genes of length &lt; 50aa and genes identified as transposable elements were filtered out of the dataset. tRNAscan-SE was used to predict tRNAs (Chan and Lowe <a href="#CR15" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR15">2019</a>). Finally, the funannotate annotate function was used to annotate genes using Eggnog (Huerta-Cepas et al. <a href="#CR43" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR43">2019</a>), Pfam (Mistry et al. <a href="#CR63" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR63">2021</a>), carbohydrate-degrading enzymes (CAZYme) (Drula et al. <a href="#CR27" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR27">2022</a>), Interproscan (Blum et al. <a href="#CR11" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR11">2021</a>), Gene Ontology (GO) terms (Gene Ontology Consortium <a href="#CR36" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR36">2004</a>), MEROPS (Rawlings et al. <a href="#CR78" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR78">2018</a>), and Secreted proteins (Choi et al. <a href="#CR20" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR20">2010</a>). Biosynthetic gene clusters (BGCs) were predicted in the two yeast genomes via fungiSMASH, the fungal-genome-specific version of antiSMASH (Blin et al. <a href="#CR10" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR10">2021</a>). We next run the funannotate compare function in 7 <em>Naganishia</em> species (which ones) and in 5 <em>Cystobasidium</em> (which ones), to look for genomic trends of the above protein categories in those two groups of fungi. Next, we ran CAFE5 to infer changes in the size of Interpro gene families regarding their phylogenetic distribution using the gene birth and death procedure to infer gene gain and loss of specific yeast phenotypes. Assembled genomes and its respectively annotations were deposited at the National Center for Biotechnology Information (NCBI; Chander et al. <a href="#CR17" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR17">2022a</a>).</p></section><section id="Sec7"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Multi-locus phylogenetic analyses</h3>
<p id="Par83">Following recent multi-gene analyses of basidiomycetous yeasts (Liu et al. <a href="#CR56" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR56">2015</a>; Wang et al. <a href="#CR101" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR101">2015b</a>), MLSA was used to obtain solid placement of the two yeast strains. The gene sequences of all available <em>Naganishia</em> strains were retrieved from NCBI, except for <em>N. tulchinskyi</em> (Bijlani et al. <a href="#CR6" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR6">2022</a>) and FJI-L2-BK-P3<sup>T</sup> strains, which were obtained from using blast tools (Additional file <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">1</a>: Table S1). Likewise, the gene sequence of all available <em>Cystobasidium</em> strains were retrieved from NCBI, except for the FKI-L6-BK-PAB1<sup>T</sup> strain, which was obtained from its WGS during this study (Additional file <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">1</a>: Table S2). Phylogenetic analyses were carried out using seven genes (ITS<em>,</em> LSU<em>,</em> SSU<em>, RPB</em>1, <em>RPB</em>2, <em>CYTB</em>, and <em>TEF1</em>) with all available <em>Naganishia</em> (<em>n</em> = 20) and <em>Cystobasidium</em> species (<em>n</em> = 21). The individual gene sequences for <em>Cystobasidium</em> and <em>Naganishia</em> datasets were aligned separately using the R package DECIPHER (Wright <a href="#CR102" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR102">2020</a>). Briefly, sequences from a fasta file were extracted and loaded using <em>ReadDNAStringSet</em> function. The <em>removeGaps</em> argument was set to "all" to remove all gaps in the input sequences. For this reason, <em>OrientNucleotides</em> function was used to reorient sequences by changing their directionality and/or complementarity to match specified reference sequences in the same set. By default, the first sequence with maximum width was used as a reference sequence. Character string indicating the allowed reorientation of non-reference sequences was set to "<em>both</em>" (for reverse complement). The algorithm works by finding the <em>k-mer</em> distance between the reference sequence. The more the sequences look similar, smaller is the evolutionary distance. Numeric threshold giving the decrease in <em>k-mer</em> distance required to adopt the alternative orientation was set to 0.05. <em>AlignSeqs</em> function was performed for the generation of the alignment. The profile-to-profile method aligns a sequence set by merging profiles along a guide tree until all the input sequences are aligned. Guide tree was automatically constructed based on a distance matrix of shared <em>k-mers</em> using the setting <em>guideTree</em> = NULL. <em>iterations</em> = 2 and <em>refinement</em> = 1 were set to specify the number of iteration steps to regenerate the guide tree based and the re-alignment. Thus, the best alignment was chosen based on its sum-of-pairs score. Construction of phylogenetic trees utilizing the seven gene sequences listed above for MLSA was performed via IQ-TREE v 2.1.1 using the concatenation approach (Minh et al. <a href="#CR62" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR62">2020</a>). ModelFinder approach was used for the evaluation of the best DNA substitution model (Kalyaanamoorthy et al. <a href="#CR46" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR46">2017</a>). Ultrafast bootstraps and SH-like approximate likelihood ratio test (SH-aLRT) were assumed for branch support (Anisimova et al. <a href="#CR2" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR2">2011</a>). The tree topologies were then visualized with the Interactive Tree Of Life v5 (<a href="https://itol.embl.de/" class="usa-link usa-link--external" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://itol.embl.de/</a>).</p></section><section id="Sec8"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Whole genome-based phylogenetic analyses</h3>
<p id="Par84">Phylogenomic trees based on universal barcodes for fungi were constructed following the PHYling pipeline (GitHub - stajichlab/PHYling_unified: Unified PHYling pipeline for species tree building from annotated genomes; see <a href="https://github.com/stajichlab/AAFTF" class="usa-link usa-link--external" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://github.com/stajichlab/AAFTF</a> and <a href="https://github.com/nextgenusfs/funannotate" class="usa-link usa-link--external" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://github.com/nextgenusfs/funannotate</a> for assembly and annotation steps). The protein models representing the <em>Tremellomycetes</em> class (for phylogenomic analysis of FJI-L2-BK-P3<sup>T</sup>) and the <em>Cystobasidiales</em> order (for phylogenomic analysis of FJI-L2-BK-P3<sup>T</sup>) were downloaded from the Mycocosm portal (Grigoriev et al. <a href="#CR38" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR38">2014</a>)<em>.</em> Additional unannotated genomes representing the <em>Cystobasidium</em> and <em>Naganishia</em> species were downloaded from NCBI and submitted to the same gene prediction papiline described above (See Genome Annotation and Comparative Genomic Analyses section). We used the hmmsearch tool from HMMER v3.3.2, to compare protein sequences to the fungi_odb10 database, a collection of benchmarking protein sequences that are conserved across a wide range of fungal species, and identify potential homologous sequences (Simão et al. <a href="#CR87" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR87">2015</a>). The tool utilizes a statistical model of protein sequence conservation to identify the degree of similarity between the query sequence and the protein sequences contained in the database. To create the protein alignments, the HMMER v3.3.2 hmmbuild function was employed. Spurious positions were purged with the ClipKIT v 1.3.0 tool, using the smartgap function. We then generated individual protein Maximum Likelihood (ML) phylogenetic trees employing IQ-TREE2 using partitions from each protein marker; the best protein model was calculated individually utilizing standard model selection (-m TEST option) (Kalyaanamoorthy et al. <a href="#CR46" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR46">2017</a>). Tree reconstruction was performed with the supermatrix approach (Chernomor et al. <a href="#CR19" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR19">2016</a>) and branch fidelity was tested using SH-aLRT (Anisimova and Gascuel <a href="#CR1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR1">2006</a>) and ultrafast bootstrap methods (Hoang et al. <a href="#CR42" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR42">2018</a>). The final tree for each species was visualized with FigTree v 1.4.4 software.</p></section><section id="Sec9"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">UV-C resistance</h3>
<p id="Par85">The evaluation of UV-C resistance was carried out via methods described previously (Blachowicz et al. <a href="#CR8" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR8">2019</a>). Briefly, yeast cells (n = 3 species) were counted using a hemocytometer (Double Neubauer Counting Chamber, Hausser Scientific, Horsham, PA, United States) from 7-day-old cultures grown at 25 °C on PDA. Cell suspensions were diluted in molecular grade sterile water (Fisher Scientific, Waltham, MA, United States) and ∼ 2 × 10<sup>4</sup> cells/mL were added to precision-cleaned aluminum coupons followed by drying overnight at room temperature in a bio-hood (Chander et al. <a href="#CR17" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR17">2022a</a>, <a href="#CR18" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR18">b</a>). Coupons with dried yeast cells were placed in plastic Petri dishes, without a lid, and exposed to UV-C using a Crosslinker CL-1000 (UVP Inc). The lamp was placed above the sample with an exposure time required to produce the following doses: 0, 500, 1000, 2000 and 3000 J/m<sup>2</sup>. The dose rate was 100 μW cm<sup>−2</sup>. After exposure to UV-C, 100 μL of 10% sterile polyvinyl alcohol (PVA) was applied on each coupon and dried at 37 °C for 60 min. Dried PVA along with yeast cells was peeled using sterile forceps and added to 1 mL of molecular grade sterile water. The PVA extraction step was repeated twice. Once PVA was dissolved, serial dilutions were prepared and plated on PDA in quadruplicates. Colony forming units (CFUs) were counted after 7 days of incubation at 25 °C.</p></section></section><section id="Sec10"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">TAXONOMY</h2>
<p id="Par86"><strong><em>Naganishia kalamii</em></strong> K. Venkateswaran, P. Leo &amp; N. K. Singh<strong>, sp. nov.</strong></p>
<p id="Par87">MycoBank: MB 846830.</p>
<p id="Par88"><em>Etymology</em>: After Avul Pakir Jainulabdeen Abdul Kalam, an Indian aerospace scientist in whose honor the species is named.</p>
<p id="Par89"><em>Diagnosis: N. kalamii</em> can be readily differentiated from its phylogenetically closest relative <em>N. albida</em> by the growth at 30 °C and 35 °C, whereas <em>N. albida</em> did not exhibit growth at temperatures higher than 25 °C. Since <em>N. kalamii</em> assimilate imidazole and exhibited growth at 10% NaCl, these tests can be used to differentiate <em>N. kalamii</em> from other member of the <em>N. albida</em> clade.</p>
<p id="Par90"><em>Type:</em>
<strong>USA</strong>: <em>California</em>: Pasadena, 34.1478° N, 118.1445° W, JPL-SAF cleanroom floor, where Mars 2020 mission components were assembled, isolated 17 Apr 2018, <em>Kasthuri Venkateswaran</em> (NRRL 64466 – holotype stored in a metabolically inactive state as a lyophilized culture; DSM 115730 – isotype cryopreserved in a metabolically inactive state).</p>
<p id="Par91"><em>Description:</em> After 5 d at 25 °C on PDA, streak culture white to cream-colored (becoming brownish upon aging), viscous to butyrous with a smooth surface. Margins smooth and entire, profile raised (Fig. <a href="#Fig1" class="usa-link">1</a>a). Cells round, ovoid to ellipsoidal (Fig. <a href="#Fig1" class="usa-link">1</a>a, b), 4.8 ± 0.97 μm, occurring singly or in pairs, proliferating by polar budding (Fig. <a href="#Fig1" class="usa-link">1</a>c, d). Pseudohyphae true hyphae, ballistoconidia, and sexual morph not observed. Glucose not fermented. D-glucose, D-galactose (weak and slow), D-xylose, L-arabinose, sucrose, maltose, methyl-α-D-glucoside (weak), lactose (weak), raffinose, melezitose (weak), starch, D-glucitol, D-mannitol, galactitol, myo-inositol, D-gluconate, D-glucoronate, succinate (weak), ethanol, D-glucarate, and L-malic acid. No growth on L-sorbose, D-glucosamine, D-ribose, D-arabinose, L-rhamnose, α,α-trehalose, salicin, melibiose, inulin, glycerol, erythritol, ribitol, L-arabinitol, 5-keto-gluconate, D-galacturonate, lactate, citrate, methanol, and L-tartaric and D-tartaric acids. </p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="Fig1"><h3 class="obj_head">Fig. 1.</h3>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=10422843_43008_2023_119_Fig1_HTML.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/7dde/10422843/3435fe473aa0/43008_2023_119_Fig1_HTML.jpg" loading="lazy" id="MO1" height="760" width="797" alt="Fig. 1"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/Fig1/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p><em>Naganishia kalamii</em> FJI-L2-BK-P3<sup>T</sup>
<strong>a</strong> Colonies on PDA are cream colored, round, and 30 mm in diameter. <strong>b</strong> Reverse view of colonies on PDA after 14 days of incubation at room temperature (24 °C). <strong>c</strong> Cells are not observed to cluster or form pseudohyphae. <strong>d</strong> Small vacuoles are observed, cells are round, vary widely in size, and reproduce through budding. (Scale bar = 10 µm)</p></figcaption></figure><p id="Par92">Positive growth observed on potassium nitrate, and sodium nitrite, L-lysine, and imidazole, but not on ethylamine, cadaverine, creatine, creatinine, glucosamine, imidazole, and D-tryptophan. Urea hydrolysis and Diazonium Blue B reaction positive. Also shows positive growth on 5% glucose medium with 5%, 8% and 10% (weak) NaCl (w/v). Growth absent on 50% and 60% (w/w) glucose-yeast extract agar. Starch-like compounds not produced. Growth occurs at 4, 25, 30, and at 35 °C and not at 37 °C on PDA.</p>
<p id="Par93">Differential biochemical characteristics of <em>N. kalamii</em> with other closely related <em>Naganishia</em> species are shown in Additional file <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">1</a>: Table S3.</p>
<p id="Par94"><em>Molecular characteristics</em> (holotype): nucleotide sequences of ITS and LSU (D1/D2 domains) rRNA are deposited in NCBI/EMBL under accession numbers <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/OQ107186" class="usa-link" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">OQ107186</a> and <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/OQ107185" class="usa-link" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">OQ107185</a>, respectively.</p>
<p id="Par95"><em>Ecology:</em> The only known strain was isolated from the cleanroom floor at JPL-SAF.</p>
<p id="Par96"><strong><em>Cystobasidium onofrii</em></strong> K. Venkateswaran, P. Leo &amp; N. K. Singh, <strong>sp. nov.</strong></p>
<p id="Par97">MycoBank MB: 844481.</p>
<p id="Par98"><em>Etymology</em>: Named in honour of Silvano Onofri, mycologist at the University of Tuscia, Viterbo, Italy, and a leading scientist in research on black yeasts and similar fungi in extreme habitats.</p>
<p id="Par99"><em>Diagnosis</em>: Darker pink to orange-colored colonies and the ability to grow in medium supplemented with 5% NaCl are useful to differentiate <em>C. onofrii</em> from other recognized species of <em>Cystobasidium</em>.</p>
<p id="Par100"><em>Type:</em>
<strong>USA</strong>: Florida: Cape Canaveral, 28° 23′ 48.6″ N, 80° 36′ 20.4″ W, KSC-PHSF isolated from cleanroom floor where Mars 2020 mission components were assembled, 12 Jun 2018, <em>Kasthuri Venkateswaran</em> (NRRL 64426—holotype stored in a metabolically inactive state as a lyophilized culture; DSM 114625—isotype metabolically inactive cryopreserved culture).</p>
<p id="Par101"><em>Description:</em> After 7 d at 25 °C on PDA, streak culture pink to orange (becoming brownish upon aging), butyrous with a smooth surface. Margins smooth and entire, and the profile raised (Fig. <a href="#Fig4" class="usa-link">4</a>a, b). Cells round, ovoid to ellipsoidal, 4.1 ± 0.97 μm, occurring singly or in pairs, proliferating by polar budding (Fig. <a href="#Fig4" class="usa-link">4</a>c, d). Pseudohyphae, true hyphae, ballistoconidia, and sexual morph were not observed.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="Fig4"><h3 class="obj_head">Fig. 4.</h3>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=10422843_43008_2023_119_Fig4_HTML.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/7dde/10422843/561c8b6b1769/43008_2023_119_Fig4_HTML.jpg" loading="lazy" id="MO2" height="758" width="797" alt="Fig. 4"></a></p>
