(Scale bar = 10 µm)</p></figcaption></figure><p id="Par102">Sugars not fermented: <span class="font-variant-small-caps">d</span>-glucose, <span class="font-variant-small-caps">d</span>-galactose (sometimes weak), <span class="font-variant-small-caps">l</span>-sorbose (sometimes weak), <span class="font-variant-small-caps">d</span>-xylose, <span class="font-variant-small-caps">l</span>-arabinose, sucrose, a,a-trehalose, lactose, melezitose, gentiobiose, inulin (sometimes weak), starch (weak), glycerol, ribitol, <span class="font-variant-small-caps">d</span>-glucitol (weak), <span class="font-variant-small-caps">d</span>-mannitol (weak), 5-keto-<span class="font-variant-small-caps">d</span>-gluconate, <span class="font-variant-small-caps">d</span>-gluconate, <span class="font-variant-small-caps">d</span>-glucuronate, ethanol, methanol, <span class="font-variant-small-caps">d</span>-glucarate and <span class="font-variant-small-caps">l</span>-malic acid are assimilated. No growth on salicin, melibiose, raffinose, <span class="font-variant-small-caps">l</span>-arabinitol, galactitol, myo-inositol, <span class="font-variant-small-caps">d</span>-galacturonate, <span class="font-variant-small-caps">l</span>-tartaric acid and <span class="font-variant-small-caps">d</span>-tartaric acid.</p>
<p id="Par103">No growth observed on potassium nitrate and sodium nitrite and ethylamine, but positive growth on <span class="font-variant-small-caps">l</span>-lysine exhibited. Urea hydrolysis and Diazonium Blue B reaction positive. Also shows positive growth on 5% glucose medium with 5%, 8% and 10% (weak) NaCl (w/v). Growth absent on 50% and 60% (w/w) glucose-yeast extract agar. Starch-like compounds not produced. Growth occurs at 4, 25, and 30 °C and not at 35 °C.</p>
<p id="Par104">Differentiating biochemical characteristics from other closely related <em>Cystobasidium</em> species are shown in Additional file <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">1</a>: Table S5. Among the <em>Cystobasidium</em> species, <em>C. tubakii</em> and <em>C. pallidum</em> were phylogenetically related but differ in assimilation of <span class="font-variant-small-caps">l</span>-arabinose and cellobiose, and growth in the presence of cycloheximide.</p>
<p id="Par105"><em>Molecular characteristics</em> (holotype): nucleotide sequences of ITS and LSU (D1/D2 domains) rRNA deposited in NCBI/EMBL under accession numbers <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/MT704910" class="usa-link" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">MT704910</a> and <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/OQ107184" class="usa-link" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">OQ107184</a>, respectively.</p>
<p id="Par106"><em>Ecology:</em> The only known strain was isolated from the cleanroom floor at the Kennedy Space Center (KSC-PHSF).</p></section><section id="Sec11"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">RESULTS</h2>
<section id="Sec12"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Naganishia kalamii</h3>
<section id="Sec13"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">Molecular phylogeny</h4>
<p id="Par107"><em>Naganishia kalamii</em> belongs to <em>Basidiomycota</em>, class <em>Tremellomycetes</em>, and order <em>Filobasidiales</em>. ITS sequence similarity of the strain FJI-L2-BK-P3<sup>T</sup> with the majority of <em>Naganishia</em> species was &gt; 98.5% suggesting that the ITS marker alone may not be useful to identify <em>Naganishia</em> species (see also Scorzetti et al. <a href="#CR82" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR82">2002</a>; Fonseca et al. <a href="#CR32" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR32">2011</a>). Furthermore, combined sequence analyses of both ITS and LSU fragments placed <em>N. kalamii</em> in the <em>N. albida</em> clade, in a distinct position next to <em>N. albida</em> var. <em>ovalis</em> CBS 5810<sup>T</sup> (two substitutions in the LSU). Although the <em>N. albida</em> clade is represented in the literature, collections and databases by an array of strains showing highly variable physiological and biochemical characteristics, the pair-wise distances matrix clearly shows the difficulties to discriminate the members of <em>N. albida</em> clade when only ITS and LSU markers were used (Additional file <a href="#MOESM2" class="usa-link">2</a>: Fig. S2). This problem was highlighted in the comments to the species by Fonseca et al. (<a href="#CR32" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR32">2011</a>), but in 2021 two <em>Naganishia</em> species were described largely based on sequence dissimilarity in the clade (Kachalkin et al. <a href="#CR140" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR140">2019</a>; Tan et al. <a href="#CR89" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR89">2021</a>). However, it is important to note that the analyzed protein-coding (<em>CYTB</em>, <em>TEF1, RPB1</em> and <em>RPB2</em>) individual phylogenetic trees of gene fragments other than SSU (invariable for the whole clade), LSU and ITS exhibited higher resolution in differentiating the <em>Naganishia</em> species (Additional file <a href="#MOESM2" class="usa-link">2</a>: Fig. S3–S7). Furthermore, seven-loci phylogenetic tree (Fig. <a href="#Fig2" class="usa-link">2</a>a) convincingly showed that FJI-L2-BK-P3<sup>T</sup> occupies a distinct position in the clade with high bootstrap support (100%). The novel species <em>N. kalamii</em> is most closely related to <em>N. albida</em> var. <em>ovalis</em> CBS 5810<sup>T</sup>, <em>N. albida</em> var. <em>kuetzingii</em> CBS 1926<sup>T</sup> (type of <em>Cryptococcus kuetzingii</em>), <em>N. albida</em> var. <em>albida</em> CBS 142<sup>T</sup>, <em>N. liquefaciens</em> CBS 968<sup>T</sup> and <em>N. adeliensis</em> CBS 8351<sup>T</sup> (Fig. <a href="#Fig2" class="usa-link">2</a>a). Another species <em>N. nivalis</em> was not included in the MLSA-analysis because protein-coding sequences were not available for the analysis. The tree based on conserved genes from the <em>N. kalamii</em> FJI-L2-BK-P3<sup>T</sup> genome (Fig. <a href="#Fig2" class="usa-link">2</a>b) showed that <em>N. kalamii</em> is phylogenetically distinct from <em>N. albida</em> var. <em>albida</em> CBS 142<sup>T</sup> and <em>Naganishia</em> sp. NRRL Y-1402 that was earlier misidentified as <em>N. albida</em> var. <em>albida</em>.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="Fig2"><h5 class="obj_head">Fig. 2.</h5>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=10422843_43008_2023_119_Fig2_HTML.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/7dde/10422843/c45ee8c0edcd/43008_2023_119_Fig2_HTML.jpg" loading="lazy" id="MO3" height="407" width="784" alt="Fig. 2"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/Fig2/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p><strong>a</strong> Maximum likelihood phylogenetic tree, based on concatenated seven gene sequences (<em>ITS, LSU, SSU, CYTB, RPB1, RPB2, TEF1</em>), extracted from WGS of the respective type strains (superscript T) or from the available genomes, showing the phylogenetic relationship of <em>N. kalamii</em> with closely related members of <em>Tremellomycetes</em>. The gene fragment lengths in base pairs (bp) are given in parenthesis: ITS (621 bp); LSU (611 bp); SSU (517 bp); <em>CYTB</em> (339 bp); <em>RPB1</em> (317 bp); <em>RPB2</em> (1282 bp); <em>TEF1</em> (1084 bp). Bootstrap values from 1000 replications are shown at branch points. Bar = 0.1 substitution per site. GenBank accession numbers of the sequences are listed in Additional file <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">1</a>: Table S1. <strong>b</strong> WGS Maximum likelihood phylogeny of <em>Naganishia kalamii</em> reconstructed using a concatenated alignment of universally distributed yeast genes. Branch fidelity used two different approaches, Ultrafast bootstraps and Gene Concordance Factors, which were added next to its corresponding branches</p></figcaption></figure></section><section id="Sec14"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">Genomic characteristics of <em>N. kalamii</em> FJI-L2-BK-P3<sup>T</sup> and comparative genomic analysis</h4>
<p id="Par108">The estimated genome size of <em>N. kalamii</em> FJI-L2-BK-P3<sup>T</sup> is 27.86 Mb and assembled into 478 scaffolds (N50 = 190,774, L50 = 38) with a largest scaffold as 1,000,601 bp. The GC content is 53.9% and the raw sequences are submitted with an accession #: <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/JAKLLX000000000" class="usa-link" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">JAKLLX000000000</a>. A total of 8,085 genes were identified, divided into 7902 mRNAs and 183 tRNAs. We have found 1,904 GO annotated and 6007 Eggnog terms, 2548 Interpro and 4783 Pfam domains, 177 CAZYmes, 219 MEROPS peptidases, 396 secreted proteins, and 1624 BUSCO orthologs. When compared with other <em>Naganishia</em> species, <em>N. kalamii</em> has the highest number of genes (8085) and 7902 proteins products (Additional file <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">1</a>: Table S4). Among the seven species of <em>Naganishia</em> analyzed, three species belonging to the <em>N. albida</em> clade (<em>N. kalamii</em>, <em>N. albida</em> JCM 2334 and NRRL Y-1402) had similar number of predicted genes and proteins, of which <em>N. kalamii</em> exhibited the highest number of secreted proteins (Additional file <a href="#MOESM2" class="usa-link">2</a>: Fig. S8) as well the number of peptidases (MEROPS; Additional file <a href="#MOESM2" class="usa-link">2</a>: Fig. S9) but not CAZymes (Fig. <a href="#Fig3" class="usa-link">3</a>). Lowest numbers of CAZymes terms were seen in <em>Naganishia</em> sp. N6 (168; deep sea isolate) and <em>N. vishniacii</em> ANT03-052 (170; Antarctic soil isolate), that were isolated from natural extreme environments.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="Fig3"><h5 class="obj_head">Fig. 3.</h5>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=10422843_43008_2023_119_Fig3_HTML.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/7dde/10422843/53502b49a6a7/43008_2023_119_Fig3_HTML.jpg" loading="lazy" id="MO4" height="439" width="784" alt="Fig. 3"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/Fig3/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>Comparative genomic analyses. <strong>a</strong> Phylogenetic tree showing gain and loss of Interpro domains of <em>Naganishia kalamii</em> and other <em>Naganishia</em> species. <strong>b</strong>–<strong>c</strong> Bar plot showing CAZYme group and family domains among the <em>Naganishia</em> species. <strong>d</strong>–<strong>e</strong> UpSet plot showing the distribution (shared and unique) of the CAZYme family terms among the <em>Naganishia</em> species and the relative table</p></figcaption></figure><p id="Par109">Based on the detailed research in the MEROPS peptide database, 219 different membrane associated peptidases were identified within the families: aspartic (10), cycteine (52), metallo (76), serine (62), threonine (17) and protease inhibitors (2). Serine peptidase, S9 prolyl oligopeptidase (S09X subfamily), and the prolyl aminopeptidases (S33) were the most abundant families detected in the <em>Naganishia</em> species. Moreover, the metallo protease thimet oligopeptidase (M03A), pitrilysin (M16A), aminopeptidase N (M01) and the serine protease <span class="font-variant-small-caps">d</span>-Ala-<span class="font-variant-small-caps">d</span>-Ala carboxypeptidase B (S12) were found to be overrepresented in the genome of <em>N. kalamii</em> (Additional file <a href="#MOESM2" class="usa-link">2</a>: Fig. S9).</p>
<p id="Par110">Detailed analysis into CAZy database revealed 49 genes associated with assembly (GT: glycosyltransferase) and 109 (84 GH: glycoside hydrolase, 3 PL: polysaccharide lyase, 22 CE: carbohydrate esterase) involved in the breakdown of carbohydrate complexes (Fig. <a href="#Fig3" class="usa-link">3</a>). In addition, 16 genes coding for initial lignin degradation (AA: auxiliary activities) and 5 involved in carbohydrate-binding module (CBM) were also predicted. According to genome-wide comparison, <em>N. kalamii</em> shared 59 CAZy families with other <em>Naganishia</em> species analyzed. No unique CAZy families have been recorded for <em>N. kalamii</em>. However, the polysaccharide lyase family 36 (PL36) was shared only among the members of the <em>N. albida</em> clade. GH5 and CE10 were the largest family (both with 15 predicted genes in <em>N. kalamii</em>) shared also by the other <em>Naganishia</em> members. GH5 consisted of genes related to cellulolytic activity while genes encoding CE10 family members act on non-carbohydrate substrates. Genes involved in the degradation of endogenous fungal cell wall polysaccharides (GH128) and lignin (AA1) were also detected.</p>
<p id="Par111">Since survival of yeasts on UV-C exposure was performed during this study, Pfam and Interpro terms associated with dehydration, desiccation, rehydration and UV resistance have been investigated (Additional file <a href="#MOESM2" class="usa-link">2</a>: Fig. S10). Results did not reveal significant differences in Pfam terms among the <em>Naganishia</em> species analyzed. Protein tyrosine and serine/threonine kinase (PF07714), WD domain, G-beta repeat (PF00400), Ras family (PF00071), Cytochrome P450 (PF00067), Ankyrin repeats (PF12796) and ABC transporter (PF00005) were the most presented Pfam terms in all the species. Heat shock protein 70 and dehydration stress-response Pfam terms were also commonly present in all the species analyzed. The psychrophilic <em>N. vishniacii</em> ANT03-052 showed presence of genes involved in raffinose synthesis (PF05691).</p>
<p id="Par112">The Interpro analysis filtered for UV resistance terms for <em>N. kalamii</em> showed the absence of protein directly involved in the synthesis of carotenoids (IPR004294). <em>N. kalamii</em> also lack the UV radiation resistance protein (IPR018791), short coiled – coil protein (IPR019357), and the XPG conserved site (IPR019974) involved in the catalytic mechanism of DNA excision repair, but XPG, N-terminal (IPR006085) and XPG/Rad2 endonuclease (IPR006084) are present, both not conserved residues. The only strain exhibiting all the terms involved in the UV resistance was Antarctic <em>N. vishniacii</em> ANT03-052 strain.</p>
<p id="Par113">The profile of fungal-specific transcriptional factors was compared among the <em>Naganishia</em> species (Additional file <a href="#MOESM2" class="usa-link">2</a>: Fig. S11). Fungal Zn(2)-Cys(6) binuclear cluster domain (IPR001138), fungal-specific TF domain (IPR007219), basic region leucine zipper 2 (IPR004827) and bZIP TF 1 (IPR004827) were commonly enriched in all the species considered. However, it was not prominent in <em>N. kalamii</em> when compared to the Antarctic strain <em>N. vishniacii</em> ANT03-052 and avian guano in South Africa strain <em>N. randhawae</em> eABCC1.</p>
<p id="Par114">CAFE5 analysis revealed that <em>N. kalamii</em> has experienced an expansion of 10 Interpro domain categories while a reduction of 43 Interpro domains (<em>p</em> &lt; 0.05) was witnessed (Fig. <a href="#Fig3" class="usa-link">3</a>a). The GO term conversion of the acquired Interpro domains are related to protein N-linked glycosylation and inositol phosphate dephosphorylation biological processes. In contrary, the contracted GO terms are related to regulation of DNA-templated transcription, regulation of transcription by RNA polymerase II, protein folding, proteolysis, ubiquitin-dependent protein catabolic process, intracellular protein transport and vesicle-mediated transport. This suggested that <em>N. kalamii</em> have lost domains related to the control of transcription as well as protein degradation, folding and transport.</p>
<p id="Par115">Six biosynthetic gene clusters (BGCs) were predicted in the genome of <em>N. kalamii</em> FJI-L2-BK-P3<sup>T</sup>, five of which do not match with any known BGCs. These included three non-ribosomal peptide synthase-like (NRPS-like) clusters, one cluster encoding for a Type III polyketide synthase, and two clusters encoding for terpene synthesis, one of which was identified as the BGC for the synthesis of clavaric acid. Similar sets of BGCs were predicted for the genomes of <em>Naganishia</em> sp. strain N6 and <em>N. albida</em> JCM 2334, with two differences: one, neither of the terpene synthesis clusters were a match for clavaric acid in the genome of <em>N. albida</em> JCM 2334; and two, <em>Naganishia</em> sp. strain N6 was predicted to have a NRPS cluster in place of one of the three NRPS-like clusters (Fig. <a href="#Fig4" class="usa-link">4</a>).</p></section></section><section id="Sec15"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Cystobasidium onofrii</h3>
<section id="Sec16"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">Phylogenetic placement</h4>
<p id="Par116">This novel yeast belongs to the phylum <em>Basidiomycota</em>, class <em>Cystobasidiomycetes</em>, and order <em>Cystobasidiales.</em> According to the phylogenetic analysis based on ITS and LSU sequences, <em>C. onofrii</em> is distantly related to <em>C. benthicum</em> JCM 10901<sup>T</sup> (16 base substitutions and 6 gaps, 97.01% of similarity in ITS and 5 base substitutions, 99.22% in LSU). The similarity of <em>C. onofrii</em> with other <em>Cystobasidium</em> species are shown in Additional file <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">1</a>: Table S6 and the two ribosomal genetic markers were found to be good to discriminate <em>Cystobasidium</em> species. The individual phylogenetic trees using ITS<em>,</em> LSU, SSU<em>, CTYB, TEF1, RPB1, RPB2,</em> (Additional file <a href="#MOESM2" class="usa-link">2</a>: Fig. S12–S18; Additional file <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">1</a>: Table S6) were generated followed by a concatenated seven-loci phylogenetic tree (Fig. <a href="#Fig5" class="usa-link">5</a>a) for <em>Cystobasidiales</em> species. All the seven loci investigated exhibited good resolution in delimiting the analysed <em>Cystobasidium</em> species. Based on the MLSA tree, strain FKI-L6-BK-PAB1<sup>T</sup> constitutes a well-supported clade with <em>C. benthicum, C. tubakii,</em> and <em>C. pallidum,</em> each of the species representing a well-separated lineage (Fig. <a href="#Fig5" class="usa-link">5</a>a). The WGS-based analysis further supported a distinct phylogenetic position of the strain FKI-L6-BK-PAB1<sup>T</sup> (Fig. <a href="#Fig5" class="usa-link">5</a>b) and formed a triad along with <em>C. tubakii</em> 9A-1–0 and <em>C. pallidum</em> JCM 3780.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="Fig5"><h5 class="obj_head">Fig. 5.</h5>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=10422843_43008_2023_119_Fig5_HTML.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/7dde/10422843/d6101ff47625/43008_2023_119_Fig5_HTML.jpg" loading="lazy" id="MO5" height="329" width="784" alt="Fig. 5"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/Fig5/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p><strong>a</strong> Maximum likelihood phylogenetic tree, based on concatenated seven gene sequences (ITS, LSU, SSU, <em>CYTB</em>, <em>RPB1</em>, <em>RPB2</em>, <em>TEF1</em>), extracted from WGS of the respective type strains (superscript T) or from the available genomes, showing the phylogenetic relationship of <em>C. onofrii</em> with closely related members of <em>Cystobasidiomycetes</em>. The gene fragment lengths in base pairs are as given in Fig. <a href="#Fig2" class="usa-link">2</a>. Bootstrap values from 1000 replications are shown at branch points. Bar = 0.1 substitution per site. GenBank accession numbers of the sequences are listed in Additional file <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">1</a>: Table S2. <strong>b</strong> WGS Maximum likelihood phylogeny of <em>Cystobasidium onofrii</em> reconstructed using a concatenated alignment of universally distributed yeast genes. Branch fidelity used two different approaches, Ultrafast bootstraps and Gene Concordance Factors, which were added next to its corresponding branches</p></figcaption></figure></section><section id="Sec17"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">Genomic characteristics of <em>C. onofrii</em> FKI-L6-BK-PAB1<sup>T</sup>and comparative genomic analysis</h4>
<p id="Par117">The estimated genome size of <em>C. onofrii</em> FKI-L6-BK-PAB1<sup>T</sup> is 20.99 Mb and assembled into 162 scaffolds (N50 = 616,502, L50 = 10) with a largest scaffold as 3,147,288 bp. The GC content is 48.8% and the raw sequences were submitted with an Accession#: <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/JAKLNA000000000" class="usa-link" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">JAKLNA000000000</a>. The final annotation of the <em>C. onofrii</em> yielded a total of 6889 genes, divided into 6831 mRNAs and 58 tRNAs. The genome annotation revealed 2934 GO and 5820 Eggnog terms, 3930 Interpro and 4803 Pfam domains, 172 CAZYmes, 221 MEROPS peptidases, 335 secreted proteins, and 1540 BUSCO orthologs.</p>
<p id="Par118">Comparative genome analyses of <em>Cystobasidium</em> species showed that <em>C. onofrii</em> has the second highest number of genes (6889) and predicted protein products (6831) after <em>C. slooffiae</em> (Additional file <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">1</a>: Table S7). The genomic annotations revealed that <em>C. onofrii</em> FKI-L6-BK-PAB1<sup>T</sup> secreted larger number of proteins and second to <em>C. slooffiae</em> among the <em>Cystobasidium</em> species (Additional file <a href="#MOESM2" class="usa-link">2</a>: Fig. S19). Similar trend is observed for the number of secreted proteins that are peptidases (Additional file <a href="#MOESM2" class="usa-link">2</a>: Fig. S20), CAZymes (Fig. <a href="#Fig6" class="usa-link">6</a>), and MEROPS classes. However, no significant changes in the number of each group have been observed. Among the CAZymes of <em>C. onofrii</em>, 30 genes associated with assembly of glycosyltransferase (GT) and 87 genes involved in the breakdown of carbohydrate complexes (52 GH: glycoside hydrolase, 5 PL: polysaccharide lyase, 30 CE: carbohydrate esterase) were predicted. In addition, 20 genes coding for initial lignin degradation (AA: auxiliary activities) and 1 involved in carbohydrate-binding module (CBM) were also noticed. According to genome-wide comparison, <em>C. onofrii</em> shared 52 CAZy families with the other 4 <em>Cystobasidium</em> species while PL Family 36 (PL36) was unique in <em>C. onofrii</em> (Fig. <a href="#Fig6" class="usa-link">6</a>). GH5 and CE10 were the largest family (both with 16 predicted genes in <em>C. onofrii</em>) shared also by the other <em>Cystobasidium</em> members. GH5 presents genes related to the degradation of cellulose while genes encoding CE10 family members act on non-carbohydrate substrates. CE family 16 is another family well represented in <em>C. onofrii</em> along with <em>C. tubakii</em> and <em>C. slooffiae.</em></p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="Fig6"><h5 class="obj_head">Fig. 6.</h5>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=10422843_43008_2023_119_Fig6_HTML.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/7dde/10422843/472a7e29351d/43008_2023_119_Fig6_HTML.jpg" loading="lazy" id="MO6" height="436" width="784" alt="Fig. 6"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/Fig6/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>Comparative genomic analyses. <strong>a</strong> Phylogenetic tree showing gain and loss of Interpro domains of <em>Cystobasidium onofrii</em> and other <em>Cystobasidium</em> species. <strong>b</strong>–<strong>c</strong> Bar plot showing CAZYme group and family domains among the <em>Cystobasidium</em> species. <strong>d</strong>–<strong>e</strong> UpSet plot showing the distribution (shared and unique) of the CAZYme family terms among the <em>Cystobasidium</em> species and the relative table</p></figcaption></figure><p id="Par119">Since <em>C. onofrii</em> FKI-L6-BK-PAB1<sup>T</sup> strain could utilize several organic substrates, a search in the MEROPS peptide database was performed in order to survey the genome for membrane associated peptidases that could initiate the degradation of complex protein molecules. In total, 221 different peptidases were identified, which were classified within the families: aspartic (13), cycteine (48), metallo (68), serine (76), threonine (19), protease inhibitors (2) and mixed peptidase (1). Serine and metallo peptidase are the predominant families in all the <em>Cystobasidium</em> species. In <em>C. onofrii</em> S9 prolyl oligopeptidase (S09X subfamily) and the prolyl aminopeptidases (S33) are the more abundant terms. S33 includes some hydrolases, which additionally degrade other substrates like lipids. The <em>exo</em>-keratinolytic enzymes M38 and the A01A subfamily members, are found abundantly in <em>C. onofrii</em> compare to the other <em>Cystobasidium</em> species (Additional file <a href="#MOESM2" class="usa-link">2</a>: Fig. S20).</p>
<p id="Par120">Pfam and Interpro terms associated with dehydration, desiccation, rehydration and UV resistance have been investigated as stress parameters (Additional file <a href="#MOESM2" class="usa-link">2</a>: Fig. S21). Results did not reveal significant differences in Pfam terms among the <em>Cystobasidium</em> analyzed. Protein tyrosine and serine/threonine kinase (PF07714), WD domain, G-beta repeat (PF00400), Ras family (PF00071), Cytochrome P450 (PF00067), Ankyrin repeats (3 copies) (PF12796) and ABC transporter (PF00005) are the most presented Pfam terms in all the species.</p>
<p id="Par121">Heat shock protein 70 and other Pfam terms for predicted proteins involved in the dehydration stress-response are also commonly present in all the <em>Cystobasidium</em> species analyzed. The Interpro analysis filtered for UV resistance terms, seen in the genome of <em>C. onofrii</em> of proteins related to the synthesis of carotenoids (IPR004294; IPR002060) and DNA-repair (eg. IPR006084). However, <em>C. onofrii</em> lacks the UV radiation resistance protein (IPR018791) and short coiled protein (IPR019357) but both of these proteins are present in the genome of the Antarctic <em>C. ongulense</em> and in <em>C. slooffiae</em> (Additional file <a href="#MOESM2" class="usa-link">2</a>: Fig. S21).</p>
<p id="Par122">Fungal-specific transcriptional factors among the <em>Cystobasidium</em> species (Additional file <a href="#MOESM2" class="usa-link">2</a>: Fig. S22) showed presence of Fungal Zn(2)-Cys(6) binuclear cluster domain (IPR001138), Fungal-specific TF domain (IPR007219), Basic region leucine zipper 2 (IPR004827), and bZIP TF 1 (IPR004827) and are more prominent in <em>C. onofrii</em> (Additional file <a href="#MOESM2" class="usa-link">2</a>: Fig. S22), when compared with the strains isolated from natural extreme environments (Antarctic soil and deep sea).</p>
<p id="Par123">Data from CAFE analysis of <em>Cystobasidium</em> species showed that <em>C. onofrii</em> has experienced an expansion of 31 Interpro domain categories while a reduction of 9 Interpro domains (<em>p</em> &lt; 0.05) (Fig. <a href="#Fig6" class="usa-link">6</a>a). The expansions were related to acetyl-CoA biosynthetic process from pyruvate, DNA repair, regulation of DNA-templated transcription, protein phosphorylation, nitrogen compound metabolic process, mono-atomic ion transport, phospholipid biosynthetic process, iron ion transmembrane transport, and positive regulation of transcription by RNA polymerase II. In contrary, the contracted GO terms were related to DNA repair, sulfate transport, and transmembrane transport.</p>
<p id="Par124">A single BGC, an NRPS-like cluster of unknown function, was predicted in the genome of <em>C. onofrii</em> FKI-L6-BK-PAB1<sup>T</sup>. The genomes of neighboring species <em>C. tubakii</em> JCM 31526<sup>T</sup> and <em>C. pallidum</em> JCM 3780<sup>T</sup> also contained a single NRPS-like cluster, with an additional unknown cluster encoding for terpene synthesis predicted for the genome of <em>C. pallidum</em> JCM 3780<sup>T</sup><em>.</em> The genome of <em>C. benthicum</em> was not available for comparison.</p></section></section><section id="Sec18"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Resistance to UV irradiation of the novel yeasts</h3>
<p id="Par125">Survival rates of two novel species and the high-altitude cold-adapted <em>N. onofrii</em> exposed to UV-C are presented in Fig. <a href="#Fig7" class="usa-link">7</a>. The cultivability of all three yeasts dramatically decreased due to desiccation before exposing them to UV-C irradiation conditions (Additional file <a href="#MOESM2" class="usa-link">2</a>: Fig. S23). The survival of yeasts after desiccation was 22% for <em>N. kalamii</em>, 23% for <em>C. onofrii</em>, and 33% for <em>N. onofrii</em>. This observed trend might be not only due to the desiccation but also the effect of polyvinyl alcohol (PVA) that was used to peel off the dried cells could not be ruled out. The number of cultivable cells after desiccation and PVA treatment were considered as baseline (N<sub>0</sub>) while calculating the effect of different doses of radiation (N).</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="Fig7"><h4 class="obj_head">Fig. 7.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><img class="graphic" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/7dde/10422843/773903c7cd9d/43008_2023_119_Fig7_HTML.jpg" loading="lazy" id="MO7" height="465" width="598" alt="Fig. 7"></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/Fig7/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>Survivability of various yeasts exposed to UV-C radiation (doses: 0, 500, 1000, 2000 and 3000 J/m<sup>2</sup>. UV-C survival rates calculated using formula: N/N<sub>0</sub>, where N is the number of cells surviving at any given dose and N<sub>0</sub> the number of cells of original seed culture (~ 2 × 10<sup>4</sup> cells/ml). The ratio of survival rates from three different species are plotted. The gray box shows the effect of desiccation and PVA treatment. The experiments were repeated twice</p></figcaption></figure><p id="Par126">In general, all three yeasts displayed a similar resistance pattern up to 2000 J/m<sup>2</sup> with a gradual increase of mortality recorded with increasing radiation doses (Fig. <a href="#Fig7" class="usa-link">7</a>). Among the three yeasts tested, only <em>N. onofrii</em> exhibited higher resistance (~ 5.5 × 10<sup>3</sup> CFU at 2000 J/m<sup>2</sup> and ~ 4.7 × 10<sup>3</sup> CFU at 3000 J/m<sup>2</sup> [<em>p</em> &lt; 0.05], with 72% of conidia were cultivable after exposure to the highest dosage [3000 J/m<sup>2</sup>]). Even though <em>C. onofrii</em> (~ 2.1 × 10<sup>3</sup> CFU) and <em>N. kalamii</em> (~ 2.9 × 10<sup>3</sup> CFU) exhibited a linear decrease in cultivability up to 2000 J/m<sup>2</sup>, a significant decrease in viability was noticed at the 3000 J/m<sup>2</sup> dosage for both <em>C. onofrii</em> (~ 5.6 × 10<sup>2</sup> CFU; <em>p</em> &lt; 0.05; Additional file <a href="#MOESM2" class="usa-link">2</a>: Fig. S23) and <em>N. kalamii</em> (~ 1.2 × 10<sup>2</sup> CFU; <em>p</em> &lt; 0.005; Additional file <a href="#MOESM2" class="usa-link">2</a>: Fig. S24). The pink-colored <em>C. onofrii</em> retained 27% of the cultivability after UV-C radiation exposure whereas the non-pigmented <em>N. kalamii</em> was the most sensitive, with only 13% of cultivable cells present after 3000 J/m<sup>2</sup> of UV-C irradiation. Exposure experiments were repeated twice and showed the same trends.</p></section></section><section id="Sec19"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">DISCUSSION</h2>
<p id="Par127">Microorganisms associated with NASA spacecraft assembly cleanrooms might hitchhike on spacecraft surfaces, contaminate extraterrestrial environments, or compromise the life-detection missions. Due to stringent maintenance and cleaning, SAFs can be considered as extreme environments since microorganisms found in these cleanrooms could withstand unfavorable conditions and survive low nutrient availability, desiccation, controlled temperature, etc. Indeed, bacteria and fungi isolated from these SAFs are poly-resistant and have been reported to withstand harsh and unfavorable conditions. Therefore, characterization of yeast species from NASA spacecraft assembly cleanrooms is essential.</p>
<p id="Par128">The identification of yeasts has been shifted from phenotypic characterization to sequence-based identification and DNA-markers were utilized for defining their phylogenetic affiliation (Fonseca et al. <a href="#CR31" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR31">1999</a>; Scorzetti et al. <a href="#CR82" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR82">2002</a>). Since the 2000s, <em>Naganishia</em> species have been identified by considering differences among ribosomal gene sequences (ITS and partial LSU), which sometimes leads to ambiguous identification in subsequent revisions of the genus (Turchetti et al. <a href="#CR95" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR95">2015</a>; Ruiz and Radwan <a href="#CR80" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR80">2021</a>). Circumscription of the species <em>Cryptococcus albidus</em> has a long and complicated history of splitting and lumping using the ever-growing array of available tests and tools, including whole-cell protein patterns, DNA-DNA hybridization, and nucleotide sequencing (Fonseca et al. <a href="#CR32" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR32">2011</a>). The species has long been known as a variable species and included, as varieties, yeasts that are presently placed in other genera and families of <em>Filobasidiales</em> (Fonseca et al. <a href="#CR32" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR32">2011</a>; Liu et al. <a href="#CR56" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR56">2015</a>). As the result of reclassification of the <em>C. albidus</em> species complex using LSU and later ITS sequences, many previous varieties were promoted to the taxonomic level of species, e.g. <em>Naganishia diffluens</em>, <em>N. globosa</em>, <em>N. liquefaciens</em>, <em>Solicoccozyma terrea</em>, <em>Solicoccozyma terricola</em>. Delineation of species using a single ribosomal gene region is not always reliable in basidiomycetous yeasts, because different lineages strongly vary in their rates of nucleotide substitution (Fell et al. <a href="#CR30" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR30">2000</a>; Scorzetti et al. <a href="#CR82" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR82">2002</a>). The case of the genus <em>Naganishia</em> is challenging as species were delimited based on very few substitutions in the two fragments, ITS and LSU. Based on ITS and LSU, the <em>albida</em> clade of the genus <em>Naganishia</em> currently include three varieties of <em>N. albida</em> (<em>N. albida</em> var. <em>albida</em> CBS 142<sup>T</sup>, <em>N. albida</em> var. <em>kuetzingii</em> CBS 1926<sup>T</sup> (formerly <em>Cryptococcus kuetzingii</em>), <em>N. albida</em> var. <em>ovalis</em> CBS 5810<sup>T</sup>) and three other species (<em>N. nivalis</em> DBVPG 5706<sup>T</sup>, <em>N. liquefaciens</em> CBS 968<sup>T</sup>, <em>N. brisbanensis</em> BRIP 28244; Additional file <a href="#MOESM2" class="usa-link">2</a>: Fig. S1; Scorzetti et al. <a href="#CR82" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR82">2002</a>; Kurtzman et al. <a href="#CR49" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR49">2011</a>; Li et al. <a href="#CR54" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR54">2020</a>). However, according to our results, combined sequence analysis of the LSU and ITS sequences, it is not possible to differentiate members of <em>N. albida</em> clade using the approach due to low discriminatory power and lack of bootstrap support (Additional file <a href="#MOESM2" class="usa-link">2</a>: Fig. S1). Our results suggest that the recently described <em>N. brisbanensis</em> (Tan et al. <a href="#CR89" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR89">2021</a>) might be a taxonomic synonym of <em>Cryptococcus kuetzingii</em>, the species that have not been included in the original analysis by Tan et al. (<a href="#CR89" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR89">2021</a>). To improve as much as possible poor species resolution in the ITS-LSU tree, a seven-loci (MLSA) and WGS-based phylogenies were generated for the discrimination of <em>N. albida</em> clade members. The MLSA tree convincingly separated <em>N. kalamii</em> from the other members of the <em>N. albida</em> clade suggesting that it should be placed in a new species rather than keeping as a <em>N. albida</em> variety which would further increasing the heterogeneity of the species. Furthermore, our MLSA- and WGS-based trees suggest that all the varieties of the <em>N. albida</em> clade are likely to be independent species (Fig. <a href="#Fig2" class="usa-link">2</a>a). However, the complete reclassification of <em>N. albida</em> must include (either as the seven-gene dataset, but preferably WGS) recently described <em>N. nivalis</em> and <em>N. brisbanensis</em>, the varieties <em>N. albida</em> var. <em>kuetzingii</em> and <em>N. albida</em> var. <em>ovalis</em>, as well as some present synonyms of the <em>N. albida</em>, namely <em>Torulopsis rotundata</em> and <em>Torulopsis nadaensis</em> (Fonseca et al. <a href="#CR32" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR32">2011</a>). In addition, WGS-based phylogeny of the strains NY-1402, N6, and I2-R1 from public databases revealed that strain NY-1402 is potentially a novel species and two other strains (N6, isolated from deep-sea sediment and I2-R1, isolated from an ISS HEPA filter for a rodent experiment) should be assigned to <em>N. tulchinskyi</em>. These examples show that MLSA and WGS analyses are necessary to avoid misidentification (Taylor and Fisher <a href="#CR91" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR91">2003</a>; Liu et al. <a href="#CR56" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR56">2015</a>; Wang et al. <a href="#CR100" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR100">2015a</a>, <a href="#CR101" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR101">b</a>; Vu et al. <a href="#CR98" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR98">2016</a>).</p>
<p id="Par129"><em>Naganishia</em> strains phylogenetically close to <em>N. kalamii</em> are well-adapted to cold environments. Extreme conditions might have a selective pressure leading to an increase in mutation rates, allowing microbial species to adapt to extreme conditions (Turchetti et al. <a href="#CR95" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR95">2015</a>). Most of the <em>Naganishia</em> species (<em>N. vaughanmartiniae</em>, <em>N. onofrii</em>, <em>N. friedmannii</em>, <em>N. cerealis</em>, <em>N. randhawae</em>, <em>N. globosa, N. adeliensis</em>, <em>N. liquefaciens</em> and <em>N. albida</em>) are able to grow at low temperatures (4 °C) but only <em>N. kalamii</em> and <em>N. qatarensis</em> can grow at 35 °C. All the psychrophilic or psychrotolerant <em>Naganishia</em> species exhibit the presence of cold-adapted genes which might have allowed them to colonize cold environments such as Antarctica and the deep sea (Selbmann et al. <a href="#CR84" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR84">2005</a>, <a href="#CR85" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR85">2013</a>; Turchetti et al. <a href="#CR95" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR95">2015</a>; Fotedar et al. <a href="#CR33" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR33">2018</a>; Han et al. <a href="#CR41" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR41">2020</a>; Segal-Kischinevzky et al. <a href="#CR83" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR83">2022</a>). <em>N. qatarensis,</em> a halophilic yeast isolated from a warm hypersaline marine environment in Qatar, is reported to grow at 30–35 °C (Fotedar et al. <a href="#CR33" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR33">2018</a>). Growth of <em>N. qatarensis</em> at lower temperature (~ 25 °C) has been reported but it took 8 weeks after incubation. Conversely, <em>N. kalamii</em> is able to grow in a temperature range of 15–35 °C after 7 days, and growth was noticed within 14 days when incubated at 4–10 °C. Similar to <em>Naganishia</em> species, the <em>C. onofrii</em> clade consists of three species that were also isolated from cold environments (Tsuji et al. <a href="#CR93" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR93">2017</a>; de Menezes et al. <a href="#CR25" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR25">2019</a>) and other members of the genus <em>Cystobasidium</em> (n = 12 species) are among the most common cold-adapted species (Buzzini et al. <a href="#CR13" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR13">2018</a>). All members of the genus <em>Cystobasidium</em>, including <em>C. onofrii</em>, are psychrophilic or psychrotolerant, with optimum growth temperatures ranging from 15 to 20 °C.</p>
<p id="Par130">Cryosphere harbors abundant microbial diversity, e.g. Antarctica, Greenland, cold lakes in Argentina, and deep sea (Buzzini and Margesin <a href="#CR14" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR14">2014</a>). Cold-adapted yeast species are reported to survive while embedded in the ice layers of glaciers or in permafrost (Zucconi et al. <a href="#CR105" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR105">2012</a>; Turchetti et al. <a href="#CR94" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR94">2013</a>), finding micro-conditions that are isolated from the surrounding environments (Price <a href="#CR75" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR75">2000</a>). Low nutrient availability is another limiting factor typical of Antarctic ecosystems. The oligotrophic Antarctic yeast <em>C. tubakii</em>, which closely related to <em>C. onofrii,</em> does not require amino acids and vitamins for growth, and is also able to grow at − 3 °C (Tsuji <a href="#CR92" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR92">2018</a>). Since future space missions—for example, ESA JUpiter ICy moons Explorer and NASA Europa Clipper missions—are designed to explore the potential habitability of icy moons, the presence of cold-adapted yeasts should be monitored in the NASA cleanrooms. For future missions, monitoring psychrophiles, xerophiles and halophiles in SAFs is of particular interest because they may be capable of surviving inside or under the icy crust of Europa due to its extremely cold and saline environment (Marion et al. <a href="#CR60" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR60">2003</a>). For instance, <em>C. lysiniphilum</em> and <em>C. slooffiae</em> are reported as halotolerant species (Mokhtarnejad et al. <a href="#CR64" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR64">2016</a>). Similarly, <em>C. onofrii</em> shows moderately halophilic aptitude since it exhibits growth at 10% NaCl.</p>
<p id="Par131">In general, SAF surfaces are decontaminated with reagents and also kept dry. Our genomic analysis reveals that both <em>Naganishia</em> and <em>Cystobasidium</em> species have predicted proteins (Pfam and Interpro terms) associated with dehydration and desiccation stress-response. Halotolerance is another adaptation required for yeasts typically active in hyper-arid regions, and so the halophilic traits detected in the genomes of these species also likely contributes to their survival in the cleanroom environment (Buzzini and Margesin <a href="#CR14" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR14">2014</a>; Logan et al. <a href="#CR57" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR57">2021</a>; Yurkov et al. <a href="#CR104" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR104">2022</a>). As reported by Yurkov et al. <a href="#CR104" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR104">2022</a>, members of both genera <em>Naganishia</em> and <em>Cystobasidium</em> (<em>N. albida</em> and <em>C. minutum</em>) were nearly the only yeasts isolated from hot and dry sandy soil of the Namib desert. <em>N. albida</em> is well-adapted to dry conditions due to a thick polysaccharides capsules, which might be responsible for its remarkable ability to survive in air-dried autoclaved loamy sand (Vishniac <a href="#CR97" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR97">1985</a>). Highly desiccation tolerance has also been reported in <em>N. vishniacii,</em> showing almost 100% survival after 27 days at a relative humidity of &lt; 1% (Connell et al. <a href="#CR24" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR24">2014</a>). Another species of the genus, <em>N. friedmannii</em> tested for desiccation (along with stratospheric low pressure and temperature and UV exposure) during stratospheric balloon flights, recorded larger numbers of viable cells than bacterial spores. Presence of the genetic traits associated with desiccation and oligotrophy were present in <em>N. kalamii</em> and <em>C. onofrii</em>, promoting their presence to the stringent conditions found in cleanrooms. Our genomic analysis revealed that along with genes related to desiccation tolerance, genes related to UV resistance are present in both <em>Naganishia</em> and <em>Cystobasidium</em> members. However, the only strain exhibiting all gene ontology terms involved in UV resistance is <em>N. vishniacii</em> ANT03-052, firstly isolated from the McMurdo Dry Valleys (Antarctica), a terrestrial Mars analogue. We may assume that local physical–chemical parameters of the environment might be one of the evolutionary drivers that induce the stabilization of the genes required to survive in the extreme conditions (Aureli et al. <a href="#CR3" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR3">2023</a>).</p>
<p id="Par132">UV-C resistance may be a critical factor for microbial survival during interplanetary transport. Most work correlates increased UV-C resistance with microbial pigmentation (Kreusch and Duarte <a href="#CR48" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR48">2021</a>). Few studies recognized the existence of non-pigmented yeasts with similar resistance (Moliné et al. <a href="#CR65" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR65">2010</a>; Schmidt et al. <a href="#CR81" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR81">2017</a>; Pulschen et al. <a href="#CR77" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR77">2018</a>; Kreusch and Duarte <a href="#CR48" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR48">2021</a>). Our results showed UV-C survival of the non-pigmented <em>N. kalamii</em> and the pigmented pink-colored <em>C. onofrii</em> at up to 3000 J/m<sup>2</sup> UV-C irradiation. Multiple species from the non-pigmented <em>Naganishia</em> species are highly resistant organisms to UV radiation (Pulschen et al. <a href="#CR76" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR76">2015</a>; Schmidt et al. <a href="#CR81" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR81">2017</a>) and our results are in line with this finding. Indeed, we found that the polar yeast <em>N. onofrii</em> is more resistant to UV-C radiation compared to the pigmented <em>C. onofrii,</em> which is capable of synthesizing carotenoids with UV shielding properties (Moliné et al. <a href="#CR65" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR65">2010</a>). Furthermore, cold-adapted yeasts, such <em>N. onofrii</em> are characterized by a thick cell wall and the ability to produce an extracellular polysaccharide capsule surrounding their cells (Turchetti et al. <a href="#CR95" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR95">2015</a>), providing robustness to the organism when exposed to stress conditions. This adaptation is typically found in extremophiles. Indeed, it is already demonstrated that deinococcal polysaccharide capsule (DeinoPol) has antioxidant properties involved in resistance to severe environmental stresses, including UV radiation and hydroxyl radicals (Lin et al. <a href="#CR150" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR150">2020</a>). During atmospheric transportation, microorganisms must cope up with not only with intense UV, but also low temperatures and dehydration (Selbmann et al. <a href="#CR84" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR84">2005</a>, <a href="#CR85" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR85">2013</a>).</p>
<p id="Par133">The serine proteases of <em>N. kalamii</em> also include some hydrolases which were reported to degrade lipids (Leger et al. <a href="#CR52" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR52">1997</a>; Muszewska et al. <a href="#CR66" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR66">2017</a>). Protein tyrosine and serine/threonine kinase (PF07714), WD domain, G-beta repeat (PF00400), Ras family (PF00071), Cytochrome P450 (PF00067), Ankyrin repeats (PF12796) and ABC transporter (PF00005) are the most abundant Pfam terms in all the <em>Naganishia</em> species and are usually associated with survival of multiple dessication-rehydration cycles (Gao et al. <a href="#CR35" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR35">2017</a>). Heat shock protein 70 and other Pfam terms for predicted proteins involved in the dehydration stress-response were also present in all the <em>Naganishia</em> species analyzed. However, only the psychrophilic <em>N. vishniacii</em> ANT03-052 had genes involved in raffinose synthesis (PF05691). Raffinose accumulation is one of the adaptive strategies involved against cold shock (Imanishi et al. <a href="#CR44" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR44">1998</a>; Egert et al. <a href="#CR28" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR28">2013</a>; Yang et al. <a href="#CR103" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR103">2022</a>).</p>
<p id="Par134">By comparing the gene conservation and functional annotation in the different species analyzed, we identified that both <em>Naganishia</em> and <em>Cystobasidium</em> species present a consistent number of genes related to a saprotrophic lifestyle of decaying organic and plant materials (PL36; GH5; CE10; GH128; AA1). Moreover, serine proteases S33 and S09X found in <em>N. kalamii,</em> are essential hydrolytic enzymes with several functions such as protein maturation, signal peptide cleavage and transduction, and intracellular protein turnover. Although both are typically understudied in yeast, they have been suggested as a marker for saprotrophy in fungi as they are theorized to be less expressed in fungi with a pathogenic or animal-associated lifestyle (Muszewska et al. <a href="#CR66" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR66">2017</a>). Furthermore, it has also been suggested that the relatively high abundance of the aminopeptidases M01 and the serine proteases S12, which are both detected in abundance in the genome of <em>N. kalamii,</em> may be an indicator of saprotrophy (Muszewska et al. <a href="#CR66" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR66">2017</a>). The same trend has been detected in <em>C. onofrii.</em> Furthermore, genes encoding a wide range of glycosyl hydrolases that have the capacity to degrade the major plant cell wall polymers (eg. AA1 for lignin and GH5 for cellulose breakdown) were found in both <em>N. kalamii</em> and <em>C. onofrii</em>. Acetyl esterases, found in <em>C. onofrii,</em> are an important component of the enzymatic machinery for fungal degradation of plant biomass and break down xylan-derived oligosaccharides and on galactoglucomannan (Venegas et al. <a href="#CR96" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR96">2022</a>). In particular, the Acetyl esterases subfamily members are preproenzymes, acting in the lysosomal/endosomal system and may be involved in the external digestion of proteins in saprophytic organisms (Chan et al. <a href="#CR16" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR16">2014</a>). The synthesis of extracellular enzymes which degrade organic matter and release nutrients is crucial for the survival of saprotrophs. However, by comparing the gene conservation and functional annotation of the two yeasts described here, we observed a loss in gene family (PFAM terms and GO-term counts) in both species, which is usually associated with a host/substrate shift from plants to animals. It is worth mentioning that the loss is more remarkable in <em>N. kalamii</em> (Muszewska et al. <a href="#CR66" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR66">2017</a>).</p></section><section id="Sec20"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">CONCLUSIONS</h2>
<p id="Par135">This study underscores the extreme, oligotrophic conditions of meticulously maintained NASA cleanrooms, which could potentially house various microbes, including significant yeast species. Traditional yeast identification methods are deemed inadequate, highlighting the need for more precise techniques like MLSA and WGS, particularly for complex genera like <em>Naganishia</em>. The study also warns about these UV-C resistant yeasts, including high-altitude, cold-adapted <em>N. onofrii</em> and two other NASA cleanroom species, to potentially contaminate extraterrestrial environments, thereby posing risks to life-detection missions. The presence of these cold-adapted yeasts in SAF bears implications for future missions to icy moons. Genomic analysis further reveals the potential for survival in harsh conditions, pointing to genes linked with desiccation tolerance and UV resistance.</p></section><section id="Sec21"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Supplementary Information</h2>

<section id="FPar2"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Adherence to national and international regulations</h3>
<p id="Par137">Not applicable.</p></section></section><section id="glossary1" class="glossary"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Abbreviations</h2>

</dl></section><section id="notes1"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Author contributions</h2>
<p>KV and NKS conceived and designed the experiments. FK coordinated in designing the sampling events. AMC supervised the standardization of experiments related to radiation/UV tolerance. KV and AMC designed and executed bright field microscopy experiments. PL, MT, JES, ACS, and NKS carried out phylogenetic analysis and alignment for positional description of organisms in the tree. AMC cultivated and incubated all strains for imaging and phenotypic characterization. PL, MT, and KV selected images and drafted descriptions. AY performed assimilation tests and provided support in the novel species descriptions. MT, JSE, ACS, and NKS analyzed the genomic data inclusive of de novo assemblies and verification, scaffold quality assessment and generated the draft assembly of the genomes. MT generated both WGS phylogenetic trees. AMC submitted genomes in NCBI. CM performed shotgun sequencing and sequence analysis using the NovaSeq platform. KV isolated the strain. PL wrote the first draft of the manuscript (Introduction, methods, results and discussion) and phylogenetic trees and figure generation. MT contributed to the final version of the manuscript. KV and ACS extensively edited the manuscript. All authors read and approved the final manuscript.</p></section><section id="notes2"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Funding</h2>
<p>The research described in this manuscript was funded by the NNH18ZDA001N-PPR award 18-PPR18-0011 to KV.</p></section><section id="notes3"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Availability of data and materials</h2>
<p>The draft genomes <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/JAKLLX000000000" class="usa-link" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">JAKLLX000000000</a> and <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/JAKLNA000000000" class="usa-link" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">JAKLNA000000000</a> and raw data (SAMN25226776 and SAMN25226807), have been deposited in GenBank under the BioProject accession number PRJNA800051. C.E.M. thanks the Scientific Computing Unit (SCU) at WCM, the WorldQuant and GI Research Foundation, NASA (80NSSC22K0254, 80NSSC23K0832, the National Institutes of Health (R01MH117406), and the LLS (MCL7001-18, LLS 9238-16, 7029-23).</p></section><section id="notes4"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Declarations</h2>

<p class="font-secondary"><em>This section collects any data citations, data availability statements, or supplementary materials included in this article.</em></p>
<section id="_adsm93_" lang="en" class="supplementary-materials"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Supplementary Materials</h3>
<section class="sm xbox font-sm" id="db_ds_supplementary-material1_reqid_"><div class="media p"><div class="caption">
<a href="/articles/instance/10422843/bin/43008_2023_119_MOESM1_ESM.pdf" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" class="usa-link">43008_2023_119_MOESM1_ESM.pdf</a><sup> (1MB, pdf) </sup><p><strong>Additional file 1</strong>: <strong>Table S1</strong>. List of accession numbers of genes used for the generation of phylogenetic trees for the strain <em>N. kalamii</em>. <strong>Table S2</strong>. List of accession numbers of genes used for the generation of phylogenetic trees for the strain <em>C. onofrii</em>. <strong>Table S3</strong>. Physiological characteristics of <em>Naganishia kalamii</em>, and closely related species. <strong>Table S4</strong>. Genomic features of the 7 Naganishia species considered in the following study. <strong>Table S5</strong>. Physiological characteristics of <em>Cystobasidium onofrii</em>, and closely related species. <strong>Table S6</strong>. The identity reports on the percentage of base pairs of the seven marker loci sequence between <em>C. onofrii</em> and all available <em>Cystobasidium strains</em>. <strong>Table S7</strong>. Genomic features of the 5 Cystobasidium species considered in the following study. <strong>Table S8</strong>. Data summary table from multiple variables. </p>
</div></div></section><section class="sm xbox font-sm" id="db_ds_supplementary-material2_reqid_"><div class="media p"><div class="caption">
<a href="/articles/instance/10422843/bin/43008_2023_119_MOESM2_ESM.pdf" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" class="usa-link">43008_2023_119_MOESM2_ESM.pdf</a><sup> (1.8MB, pdf) </sup><p><strong>Additional file 2</strong>: <strong>Fig. S1</strong>. Maximum likelihood phylogenetic tree, based on concatenated ITS and LSU gene sequences of <em>N. kalamii</em> and related <em>Naganishia</em> species, class <em>Tremellomycetes</em>, order <em>Filobasidiales</em>. The tree was rooted with <em>G. gastricus</em> type strains. Bootstrap percentages from 1000 replications are shown on the branches (values below 50.00 are not shown). GenBank accession numbers of the sequences are listed in Additional file <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">1</a>: Table S1. <strong>Fig. S2</strong>. Pairwise sequence genetic distance matrix based on LSU (below the diagonal) and ITS (above the diagonal) gene sequences of <em>N. kalamii</em> and related <em>Naganishia</em> species (albida clade highlighted). The function dist.dna in the ape: Analyses of Phylogenetics and Evolution package (R software) with the Kimura 2-parameter substitution model was used for the generation of the matrix. <strong>Fig. S3</strong>. SSU phylogenetic tree of <em>N. kalamiii</em> and related taxa of <em>N. albida </em>clade (shown only in part), class <em>Tremellomycetes</em>, order <em>Filobasidiales</em>. The tree was rooted with <em>G. gastricus</em> type strains. Bootstrap percentages from 1000 replications are shown on the branches (values below 50.00 are not shown). GenBank accession numbers of the sequences are listed in Additional file <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">1</a>: Table S1. <strong>Fig. S4</strong>. CYTB phylogenetic tree of <em>N. kalamiii</em> and related taxa of <em>N. albida</em> clade (shown only in part), class <em>Tremellomycetes</em>, order <em>Filobasidiales</em>. The tree was rooted with G. gastricus type strains. Bootstrap percentages from 1000 replications are shown on the branches (values below 50.00 are not shown). GenBank accession numbers of the sequences are listed in Additonal file <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">1</a>: Table S1. <strong>Fig. S5</strong>. TEF1 phylogenetic tree of <em>N. kalamiii</em> and related taxa of <em>N. albida</em> clade (shown only in part), class <em>Tremellomycetes</em>, order <em>Filobasidiales</em>. The tree was rooted with <em>G. gastricus</em> type strains. Bootstrap percentages from 1000 replications are shown on the branches (values below 50.00 are not shown). GenBank accession numbers of the sequences are listed in Additional file <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">1</a>: Table S1. <strong>Fig. S6</strong>. RPB1 phylogenetic tree of <em>N. kalamiii</em> and related taxa of <em>N. albida</em> clade (shown only in part), class <em>Tremellomycetes</em>, order <em>Filobasidiales</em>. The tree was rooted with <em>G. gastricus</em> type strains. Bootstrap percentages from 1000 replications are shown on the branches (values below 50.00 are not shown). GenBank accession numbers of the sequences are listed in Additional file <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">1</a>: Table S1. <strong>Fig. S7</strong>. RPB2 phylogenetic tree of <em>N. kalamiii</em> and related taxa of <em>N. albida</em> clade (shown only in part), class <em>Tremellomycetes</em>, order <em>Filobasidiales</em>. The tree was rooted with <em>G. gastricus</em> type strains. Bootstrap percentages from 1000 replications are shown on the branches (values below 50.00 are not shown). GenBank accession numbers of the sequences are listed in Additional file <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">1</a>: Table S1. <strong>Fig. S8</strong>. Comparative genomic analysis. a) Phylogenetic tree showing gain and loss Interpro domain among 7 <em>Naganishia</em> genomes and b) the relative predicted secreted proteins. <strong>Fig. S9</strong>. Comparative genomic analysis. a) Phylogenetic tree showing gain and loss of Interpro domains among 7 <em>Naganishia</em> genomes and b) the relative distribution of the predicted protease (MEROPS) groups and, the most present c) families and subfamilies. <strong>Fig. S10</strong>. Comparative genomic analysis. a) Phylogenetic tree showing gain and loss of Interpro domains among 7 <em>Naganishia</em> genomes and b) the relative distribution of the predicted proteins terms filtered by dehydration, desiccation, rehydration and UV resistance into Pfam and Interpro database. <strong>Fig. S11</strong>. Comparative genomic analysis. a) Phylogenetic tree showing gain and loss of Interpro domains among 7 <em>Naganishia</em> genomes and b) the relative distribution of the fungal-specific transcriptional factors. Factors with a standard deviation &gt;1 was plotted in a heat map. <strong>Fig. S12</strong>. ITS phylogenetic tree of C. onofrii. The tree was rooted with <em>R. mucilaginosa</em> and <em>N. sebacea</em> type strains. Bootstrap percentages from 1000 replications are shown on the branches (values below 50.00 are not shown). GenBank accession numbers of the sequences are listed in Additional file <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">1</a>: Table S2. <strong>Fig. S13</strong>. LSU phylogenetic tree of <em>C. onofrii</em>. The tree was rooted with <em>R. mucilaginosa</em> and <em>N. sebacea</em> type strains. Bootstrap percentages from 1000 replications are shown on the branches (values below 50.00 are not shown). GenBank accession numbers of the sequences are indicated after strain numbers and listed in Additional file <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">1</a>: Table S2. <strong>Fig. S14</strong>. SSU phylogenetic tree of <em>C. onofrii</em>. The tree was rooted with <em>R. mucilaginosa</em> and <em>N. sebacea</em> type strains. Bootstrap percentages from 1000 replications are shown on the branches (values below 50.00 are not shown). GenBank accession numbers of the sequences are listed in Additional file <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">1</a>: Table S2. <strong>Fig. S15</strong>. CYTB phylogenetic tree of <em>C. onofrii</em>. The tree was rooted with <em>R. mucilaginosa</em> and <em>N. sebacea</em> type strains. Bootstrap percentages from 1000 replications are shown on the branches (values below 50.00 are not shown). GenBank accession numbers of the sequences are listed in Additional file <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">1</a>: Table S2. <strong>Fig. S16</strong>. TEF1 phylogenetic tree of <em>C. onofrii</em>. The tree was rooted with <em>R. mucilaginosa</em> and <em>N. sebacea</em> type strains. Bootstrap percentages from 1000 replications are shown on the branches (values below 50.00 are not shown). GenBank accession numbers of the sequences are listed in Additional file <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">1</a>: Table S2. <strong>Fig. S17</strong>. RPB1 phylogenetic tree of <em>C. onofrii</em>. The tree was rooted with <em>R. mucilaginosa</em> and <em>N. sebacea</em> type strains. Bootstrap percentages from 1000 replications are shown on the branches (values below 50.00 are not shown). GenBank accession numbers of the sequences are listed in Additional file <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">1</a>: Table S2. <strong>Fig. S18</strong>. RPB2 phylogenetic tree of <em>C. onofrii</em>. The tree was rooted with <em>R. mucilaginosa</em> and <em>N. sebacea</em> type strains. Bootstrap percentages from 1000 replications are shown on the branches (values below 50.00 are not shown). GenBank accession numbers of the sequences are listed in Additional file <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">1</a>: Table S2. <strong>Fig. S19</strong>. Comparative genomic analysis. a) Phylogenetic tree showing gain and loss Interpro domain among 5 Cystobasidium genomes and b) the relative predicted secreted proteins. <strong>Fig. S20</strong>. Comparative genomic analysis. a) Phylogenetic tree showing gain and loss of Interpro domains among 5 Cystobasidium genomes and b) the relative distribution of the predicted protease (MEROPS) groups and, the most present c) families and subfamilies. <strong>Fig. S21</strong>. Comparative genomic analysis. a) Phylogenetic tree showing gain and loss of Interpro domains among 5 Cystobasidium genomes and b) the relative distribution of the predicted proteins terms filtered by dehydration, desiccation, rehydration and UV resistance into Pfam and Interpro database. <strong>Fig. S22</strong>. Comparative genomic analysis. a) Phylogenetic tree showing gain and loss of Interpro domains among 5 Cystobasidium genomes and b) the relative distribution of the fungal-specific transcriptional factors. Factors with a standard deviation &gt; 1 was plotted in a heat map. <strong>Fig. S23</strong>. Survivability of various yeasts exposed to UV-C radiation (doses: 0, 500, 1000, 2000 and 3000 J/m<sup>2</sup>. UV-C survival rates calculated using formula: N/N0, where N is the number of cells surviving at any given dose and N0 is the number of cultivable cells after desiccation and PVA treatment considered as baseline (0). The ratio of survival rates from three different species are plotted. The gray box shows the effect of desiccation and PVA treatment. The experiments were repeated twice. <strong>Fig. S24</strong>. Cultivation test performed in colonies exposed to UV-C radiation (doses: 0, 500, 1000, 2000 and 3000 J/m<sup>−2</sup>). Bar-plot showing number of viable cells after UV-C treatment. Significant differences were calculated by t-test with ∗<em>p</em> &lt; 0.05; ∗∗<em>p</em> &lt; 0.001; ∗∗∗<em>p</em> &lt; 0.0001 and ∗∗∗∗<em>p</em> &lt; 0.00001 (see Additional file <a href="#MOESM1" class="usa-link">1</a>: Table S7).</p>
</div></div></section></section><section id="_adda93_" lang="en" class="data-availability-statement"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Data Availability Statement</h3>
<p>The draft genomes <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/JAKLLX000000000" class="usa-link" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">JAKLLX000000000</a> and <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/JAKLNA000000000" class="usa-link" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">JAKLNA000000000</a> and raw data (SAMN25226776 and SAMN25226807), have been deposited in GenBank under the BioProject accession number PRJNA800051. C.E.M. thanks the Scientific Computing Unit (SCU) at WCM, the WorldQuant and GI Research Foundation, NASA (80NSSC22K0254, 80NSSC23K0832, the National Institutes of Health (R01MH117406), and the LLS (MCL7001-18, LLS 9238-16, 7029-23).</p></section></section></section><footer class="p courtesy-note font-secondary font-sm text-center"><hr class="headless">
